\pagebreak

\chapter{Further Linear Regression}\label{further-linear-regression}

\vspace{-1cm}

In Chapter 5 the concept of using \textit{linear regression} to explore straight-line relationships between variables, and to make predictions, was introduced. Linear regression will now be explored in much more detail.

\subsection*{The Method of Least Squares Regression}\label{the-method-of-least-squares-regression}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{The Method of Least Squares Regression}

Given bivariate data, it is \(x\) is generally selected as the \textbf{predictor} (or \textit{independent}) variable, whilst \(Y\) is a \textit{random variable} known as the \textbf{response} (or \textit{dependent}) variable. In other words, it is typical to want to be able to to predict an unknown \(Y\) value given a known \(x\) value.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.4\textwidth}
  \centering
\begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.8]
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (7,0) node[below] {$x$};
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,5) node[left] {$y$};
        \node[below] at (3.5,0) {predictor variable};
        \node[rotate=90,above] at (0,2.5) {response variable};
        \draw[very thick] (0.5,1.6) -- (5.25,4.07) node[above] {$y=\alpha+\beta x$};
        \draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.5258224,3.072553) circle (0.05) -- (3.5258224,3.173427648);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.3020558,2.331238) circle (0.05) -- (3.3020558,3.057069016);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.7090608,3.683062) circle (0.05) -- (3.7090608,3.268711616);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (4.3429324,4.169212) circle (0.05) -- (4.3429324,3.598324848);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (0.9282517,1.662861) circle (0.05) -- (0.9282517,1.822690884);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.9876255,3.250634) circle (0.05) -- (3.9876255,3.41356526);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (4.6912468,3.534086) circle (0.05) -- (4.6912468,3.779448336);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (1.8099240,2.297074) circle (0.05) -- (1.8099240,2.2811605);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (1.4651929,1.490792) circle (0.05) -- (1.4651929,2.101900308);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (2.2436259,2.614456) circle (0.05) -- (2.2436259,2.506685468);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (4.4783236,3.647896) circle (0.05) -- (4.4783236,3.668728272);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.9126398,2.929844) circle (0.05) -- (3.9126398,3.374572696);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.4281654,3.268362) circle (0.05) -- (3.4281654,3.122646008);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (1.3975460,2.601538) circle (0.05) -- (1.3975460,2.0667239);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (2.8836849,3.199075) circle (0.05) -- (2.8836849,2.839516148);
\draw[fill,densely dashed] (3.2025879,3.355931) circle (0.05) -- (3.2025879,3.005345708);
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.6\textwidth}
    The theoretical model for linear regression is that of a straight line of form $y=\alpha+\beta x$ describing the \textit{best fit} for a relationship between variables.  However, the population of bivariate data will not perfectly lie along this line due to chance variation.

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=The Linear Regression Model,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=6.5cm]
\[Y_i=\alpha+\beta x_i+\varepsilon_i\]
\end{tcolorbox}
 
$\varepsilon_i$ (\textit{"epsilon"}) are random variables called \textbf{errors} which account for observed values of $Y_i$ being greater than or less than those predicted by the regression line for any given value of $x_i$. 
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

The true values of \textbf{intercept parameter} \(\alpha\) and \textbf{slope parameter} \(\beta\) cannot be known exactly without access to the full \textit{population} of bivariate data. However the sample observations \((x_i,y_i)\) can be used to give estimates, \(a\) and \(b\), of \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\). The method of least squares gives formulae for \(a\) and \(b\) derived by considering how \(\sum \varepsilon_i^2\) might be minimised. These rely on several assumptions about the population of errors given on Page 5 of the Data Booklet, along with the formulae:

\begin{multicols}{2}

$$b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}} \qquad \text{and} \qquad a=\bar{y}-b\bar{x}$$

This leads to the relationship $\hat{Y}_i=a+bx_i$, where $\hat{Y}_i$ (\textit{"y-hat"}) denotes the expected value of $Y_i$ for a given $x_i$, and also the best \textit{`fitting'} prediction.

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Assumptions for the Errors,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=6.7cm]
\begin{center}
    \begin{itemize}
        \item $\varepsilon_i$ are independent.
        \item $\text{E}(\varepsilon_i)=0$
        \item $\text{V}(\varepsilon_i)=\sigma^2$ where $\text{V}(Y)=\sigma^2$
    \end{itemize}
\end{center}
\end{tcolorbox}

\end{multicols}

\pagebreak

\section{Calculating the Value of a Residual}\label{calculating-the-value-of-a-residual}

For any given \(x_i\), the equation of the least squares regression line \(\hat{Y_i}=a+bx_i\) gives a \textit{predicted} value \(\hat{Y_i}\), also referred to as a \textbf{fitted} value. For any data point \((x_i,y_i)\), the value of \(y_i\) may be called the \textbf{observed} value.

The difference between an observed value \(y_i\) and a corresponding fitted value \(\hat{Y_i}\) is called a \textbf{residual}:

\begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw[fill=black!10] (8.2,0.8) rectangle (13,2.3);
        \draw[very thick,dashed] (3,1) -- (3,2);
        \draw[semithick] (6,0) -- (0,2) node[left] {$\hat{Y_i}=a+bx_i$};
        \draw[fill=black] (3,2) circle (2pt) node[above] {$(x_i,y_i)$};
        \node[below] at (3,0.9) {$(x_i,\hat{Y}_i)$};
        \draw[dashed] (3,1) -- (3,2);
        \draw[fill] (3,1) circle (1pt);
        \draw[-stealth] (4.5,2) -- (3.2,2);
        \node[right] at (4.5,2) {Observed, $y_i$};
        \draw[-stealth] (4.5,1) -- (3.2,1);
        \node[right] at (4.5,1) {Fitted, $\hat{Y_i}$};
        \draw[stealth-,dashed,thin] (3.2,1.5) -- (8,1.5);
        \node at (9.2,1.9) {Residual};
        \node at (10.25,1.85) {$=$};
        \node at (10.95,1.9) {Data};
        \node at (11.67,1.85) {$-$};
        \node at (12.25,1.9) {Fit};
        \node at (10.25,1.15) {$=$};
        \node at (10.95,1.2) {$y_i$};
        \node at (11.67,1.15) {$-$};
        \node at (12.25,1.2) {$\hat{Y_i}$};
        \node at (10.7,1.55) {\begin{minipage}{6cm}
            \begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Calculating a Residual,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black]
            \vspace{-0.5cm}
\begin{align*}
    \text{Residual}&=\text{Observed}-\text{Fitted}\\
    &=\hspace{0.7cm}y_i\hspace{0.55cm}-\hspace{0.3cm}\hat{Y}_i
\end{align*}
\end{tcolorbox}
        \end{minipage}};
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\begin{multicols}{2}
    \begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.7]
        \draw[very thick] (0,0) -- (8,0.8) node[right] {$\hat{Y}=a+bx$};
        \draw[dashed] (1,1) -- (1,0.1);
        \draw[dashed] (2,-0.5) -- (2,0.2);
        \draw[thin,dashed] (3,0.7) -- (3,0.3);
        \draw[thin,dashed] (4,2) -- (4,0.4);
        \draw[thin,dashed] (5,-0.8) -- (5,0.5);
        \draw[thin,dashed] (6,1.2) -- (6,0.6);
        \draw[thin,dashed] (7,0) -- (7,0.7);
        \draw[fill] (1,1) circle (0.05);
        \draw[fill] (3,0.7) circle (0.05);
        \draw[fill] (4,2) circle (0.05);
        \draw[fill] (6,1.2) circle (0.05);
        \draw[fill] (2,-0.5) circle (0.05);
        \draw[fill] (5,-0.8) circle (0.05);
        \draw[fill] (7,0) circle (0.05);
        \node at (7,2) {\textit{positive residuals}};
        \node at (7,-1) {\textit{negative residuals}};
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Data points \textit{above} the regression line have a \textit{positive} residual: the observed $y_i$ was \textit{greater} than that predicted by the regression line for the value of $x_i$.

Data points  \textit{below} the regression line have a \textit{negative} residual: the observed $y_i$ was \textit{less} than that predicted by the regression line for the value of $x_i$.

\end{multicols}

To calculate a residual the fitted value, \(\hat{Y}_i\), needs to first be found using the equation of the regression line.

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:}

Students sitting a French exam are given a mark for each of the two components: speaking ($x$) and listening ($y$). From a random sample of 12 students, the $y$ on $x$ regression line is calculated as $\hat{Y}=4.854+0.578x$. Calculate the residual for a student who gains:\\

\text{(a)   } 20 in the speaking test and 13 in the listening test.\\[0.5em]
\text{(b)   } 16 in the speaking test and 15 in the listening test.

\tcblower

\textbf{Solution:}

\vspace{-0.5cm}

\begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \node[right] at (6.2,3.1) {(a)};
        \node[right] at (7,3.3) { $\hat{Y}=4.854+0.578\times 20=16.414$};
        \node[right] at (7,2.7) {Residual $=13-16.414=-3.414$};
        \node[right] at (6.2,1.0) {(b)};
        \node[right] at (7,1.2) { $\hat{Y}=4.854+0.578\times 16=14.102$};
        \node[right] at (7,0.6) {Residual $=15-14.102=0.898$};
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (5.2,0) node[right] {$x$};
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,5.25) node[above] {$y$}; 
        \node at (2.5,-1) {speaking};
        \node at (-1.7,2.5) {listening};
        \foreach \x in {0,5,...,20}{
        \draw (0.25*\x,0) -- (0.25*\x,-0.1) node[below] {\x};
        }
        \foreach \y in {0,5,...,20}{
        \draw (0,0.25*\y) -- (-0.1,0.25*\y) node[left] {\y};
        }
        \draw[thick] (0.25*3,0.25*3*0.578+0.25*04.854) -- (0.25*20,0.25*20*0.578+0.25*4.854);
\draw[fill] (1.75,2.25) circle (0.03);
\draw[thin, densely dashed,black!50] (5,3.25) -- (5,4.1035);
\draw[thin, densely dashed,black!50] (16*0.25,15*0.25) -- (16*0.25,0.25*14.102);
\draw[fill] (5,3.25) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3,3.25) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (4.75,4.25) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (1.5,2.25) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (4.25,3.5) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (4.25,4) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (4.5,4) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (3,3) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (1,1.5) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill] (4,3.75) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.25,2.25) circle (0.03);
\draw[thin,stealth-] (20.5*0.25,0.25*14.7) -- (5.75,4.15);
\node at (6,4.4) {$-3.414$};
\draw[thin,stealth-] (3.8,3.7) -- (3.2,4.1);
\node at (3.1,4.4) {$0.898$};
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\end{tcolorbox}

\section{Estimating the Error Variance}\label{estimating-the-error-variance}

The least squares regression model assumes that that the variance of the errors is a constant for all \(x_i\) (and \(Y_i\)):

\[\text{V}(\varepsilon_i)=\sigma^2\]

The \emph{error variance} is the variance of the population of data \emph{relative to the regression line's predicted values}, loosely indicated below by the series of arrows below.

\begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.9]
        \draw[ultra thick,stealth-stealth,black!30] (1,1*0.39+2.13+1.4) -- (1,1*0.39+2.13-1.4);
    \draw[ultra thick,stealth-stealth,black!30] (3,3*0.39+2.13+1.4) -- (3,3*0.39+2.13-1.4);
    \draw[ultra thick,stealth-stealth,black!30] (5,5*0.39+2.13+1.4) -- (5,5*0.39+2.13-1.4);
    \draw[ultra thick,stealth-stealth,black!30] (7,7*0.39+2.13+1.4) -- (7,7*0.39+2.13-1.4);
    \draw[ultra thick,stealth-stealth,black!30] (9,9*0.39+2.13+1.4) -- (9,9*0.39+2.13-1.4);
        \draw[thick] (0,2.13) -- (10,6.02) node[right] {$\hat{Y}=\alpha+\beta x$};
        \draw[fill] (6.955968,6.2978298) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (7.912889,5.8402953) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.868432,1.6709538) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.571086,5.5025033) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.498014,3.9415629) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.819003,4.6160401) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.354720,5.4286745) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.692772,3.8360525) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.919847,4.0510383) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.901206,4.4838583) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (7.173250,4.7138811) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.177491,4.5210034) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (8.281164,7.4271066) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.304496,6.2752596) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.051405,3.3392576) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.559364,3.9908928) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.048672,3.1857659) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.457014,5.0693545) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.870260,3.0534712) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.025332,2.3569438) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.603807,4.3495520) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (8.964121,5.5267531) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (8.595800,6.0630182) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.642958,2.1614941) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.288687,0.7441907) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (7.331049,3.5987320) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.517530,4.8806159) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.524255,2.6571825) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (7.153844,4.0209312) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.748901,6.1275396) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.758351,5.2985055) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.082882,3.1929715) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.964579,2.8719889) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.656324,2.0837197) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.829647,3.6616396) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.473691,5.8108632) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.814834,2.3950480) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.579615,3.8515882) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (8.730507,5.0549693) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (6.122615,4.5842622) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.604990,5.0227012) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.715744,4.0786740) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.666348,3.9671370) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (4.833494,2.6864497) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.699929,3.7831202) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (5.875923,4.1321580) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.141053,2.4180573) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.810106,4.7228958) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.983993,2.6769287) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (8.486224,3.7495115) circle (0.05);
    \node at (6,1) {constant variance of the errors $\epsilon_i$};
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

The error variance \(\sigma^2\) is estimated by:

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Estimating the Error Variance,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=6cm]
\[s^2=\dfrac{\text{\text{SSR}}}{n-2}\]
\end{tcolorbox}

Where the (always positive) \textit{sum of squared residuals}, \(\text{\text{SSR}}\), is calculated as:\hspace{0.5cm} \(\text{\text{SSR}}=S_{yy}-\dfrac{S^2_{\phantom{..}xy}}{S_{xx}}\)

Both the formula for estimating the error variance \(\sigma^2\) and the more convenient second formula for the sum of squared residuals \(\text{SSR}\) are given on page 5 of the Data Booklet.

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!,colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} 

Students sitting a French exam are given a mark for each of the two components: speaking ($x$) and listening ($y$). From a random sample of 12 students: $S_{xx}=344.9,\,S_{yy}=132.9,\,S_{xy}=193.6$.\\

\text{(a)   } Calculate the sum of squared residuals for this data set.\\[0.3em]
\text{(b)   } Obtain an estimate of the error variance.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}

\text{(a)   } $\text{SSR}=S_{yy}-\dfrac{S^2_{\phantom{..}xy}}{S_{xx}}=132.9-\dfrac{193.6^2}{344.9}=24.23$\\[0.7em]
\text{(b)   } $s^2=\dfrac{\text{SRR}}{n-2}=\dfrac{24.23}{12-2}=2.423$
\end{tcolorbox}

\pagebreak

\phantomsection\label{ex182q}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex182a]{Exercise 18.2}}{Exercise 18.2}}\label{exercise-18.2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex182a]{Exercise 18.2}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item A bivariate random sample has summary statistics:
\begin{center}
  \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
      $S_{xx}=28.92$ & \quad & $S_{yy}=495.0$ & \quad & $S_{xy}=105.3$ & \quad & $n=14$
  \end{tabular}
\end{center}
It is determined that linear relationship appears to exist between the variables. 
\begin{enumerate}
        \item Obtain an estimate $s^2$ for the error variance, $\sigma^2$.
        \item Hence determine the value of $s$ for this bivariate data.
    \end{enumerate}

\textbackslash pagebreak

\section{The Coefficient of Determination}\label{the-coefficient-of-determination}

The \emph{coefficient of determination}, \(R^2\), is the proportion of the total variation in the response variable, \(Y\), that is explained by the linear regression model. The higher the value of \(R^2\), the less variation remains for \(Y_i\) relative to the total variation of \(Y\). This means that the coefficient of determination is a very useful measure of how `good' a model is for the data.

\begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (5,0) node[right] {$x$};
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,3) node[above] {$y$};
            \node at (2.5,0.7) {$R^2=0.1246$};
            \draw[thick] (0.2,1.87+0.13*0.2) -- (4.8,1.87+0.13*4.8);
            \draw[fill=black] (0.2,1.53373841023011) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (0.4,1.20717964535332) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (0.6,1.72251067887986) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (0.8,2.11648810666721) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (1,1.97650101823779) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (1.2,1.52303291760533) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (1.4,1.58992089360673) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (1.6,2.80483627736993) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (1.8,2.74061040424513) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (2,2.84559934643717) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (2.2,2.90524254023335) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (2.4,2.38201538321828) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (2.6,1.59873854386617) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (2.8,2.90173502441664) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (3,2.59999938622566) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (3.2,2.5591860521887) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (3.4,1.76705018101233) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (3.6,1.86661649309301) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (3.8,3.00434568581749) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (4,2.29277113670136) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (4.2,2.21107850467089) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (4.4,2.02013969791051) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (4.6,2.5745743227038) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (4.8,1.98816925972809) circle (0.03);
            \draw[-stealth] (12,0) -- (17,0) node[right] {$x$};
            \draw[-stealth] (12,0) -- (12,3) node[above] {$y$};
            \node at (16,2.5) {$R^2=0.9604$};
            \draw[fill=black] (12.2,2.85798900535176) circle (0.03);
            \draw[thick] (12.2,3-0.54*0.2) -- (16.8,3-0.54*4.8);
\draw[fill=black] (12.4,2.60537287961341) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (12.6,2.77018510784324) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (12.8,2.66349862608913) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (13,2.35990060265767) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (13.2,2.30035706778736) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (13.4,2.08602522426643) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (13.6,2.25084122059611) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (13.8,2.14551272622872) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (14,1.81146604663732) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (14.2,2.08407903013156) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (14.4,1.50565270007012) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (14.6,1.46149356269359) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (14.8,1.39099215853279) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (15,1.64671625827524) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (15.2,1.21641922001981) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (15.4,1.46251390454929) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (15.6,0.938058516930767) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (15.8,0.825017334308726) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (16,0.823330020748936) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (16.2,0.687053814962354) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (16.4,0.731984300904749) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (16.6,0.450020978572811) circle (0.03);
\draw[fill=black] (16.8,0.301613948412619) circle (0.03);
\node[text width = 5.5cm,below right] at (0.1,-0.2) {12.5\% of the variation in $Y$ is explained by the linear model.};
\node[text width = 5.5cm,below right] at (12.1,-0.2) {96.0\% of the variation in $Y$ is explained by the linear model.};
\node at (8.5,1.7) {
\begin{minipage}{5.9cm}
\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Coefficient of Determination,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black]
$R^2=\dfrac{\text{explained variation}}{\text{total variation}}$
\end{tcolorbox}
\end{minipage}
};
        \end{tikzpicture}    
\end{center}

For the linear regression models covered in this course, the coefficient of determination is connected to the \emph{correlation coefficient}, \(r\), by a simple relationship:

\begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}
        \node[left] at (0,0) {$r=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{\sqrt{S_{xx}S_{yy}}}$};
        \node at (2,0) {$\implies$};
        \node[right] at (4,0){
        \begin{minipage}{2.5cm}
        \begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black]
\vspace{-0.5cm}
\[R^2=r^2\]
\end{tcolorbox}
        \end{minipage}
};
        \end{tikzpicture}    
\end{center}

Both the formula above and the interpretation of the coefficient of determination implies that \(0\leqslant R^2 \leqslant 1\).

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} 

Students sitting a French exam are given a mark for each of the two components: speaking ($x$) and listening ($y$). From a random sample of 12 students, the following summary statistics are obtained:

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{ccccc}
        $S_{xx}=334.9$ & $S_{yy}=132.9$ & $S_{xy}=193.6$ & $n=12$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

A linear regression model is proposed, and an equation for the regression line of $y$ on $x$ is obtained:

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{c}
        $\hat{Y}=4.854+0.578x$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

Calculate the coefficient of determination and interpret this value in context.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}

\vspace{-0.5cm}

\begin{align*}
r&=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{\sqrt{S_{xx}S_{yy}}}=\dfrac{193.6}{\sqrt{334.9\times 132.9}}=0.9177\\[1em]
R^2&=r^2=0.9177^2=0.842
\end{align*}

This suggests that 84.2\% of the variation in listening marks can be explained by the linear model.

\end{tcolorbox}

The conclusion from the example above could also be phrased as:

``\emph{87.4\% of the variation in listening marks can be explained by the speaking marks.}''

        \texttt{y on x regression line} & \qquad &\texttt{n} & \qquad &\texttt{r} & \qquad &\texttt{s}\\
        \texttt{top\_speed = 269.2 - 1.72 $\times$ lap\_time} && \texttt{34} && \texttt{-0.670}&& \texttt{8.49}
    \end{tabular}
\end{enumerate} 
\item A seller on an online auction site has a stock of used digital cameras, all aged somewhere between 2 and 12 years. \end{enumerate}

\pagebreak

\section{\texorpdfstring{Test for the Slope Parameter \(\beta\)}{Test for the Slope Parameter \textbackslash beta}}\label{test-for-the-slope-parameter-beta}

The true value of the \textit{slope parameter}, \(\beta\), cannot be known without obtaining a full population. The \textit{sample statistic} \(b\) is an estimator for \(\beta\), and may differ slightly or substantially from it, as shown below.

\end{multicols}

The slope parameter \(\beta\) can be \emph{positive}, \emph{negative} or \emph{zero}. Where \(\beta=0\) it can be said that knowing \(x_i\) has no value in predicting \(y_i\). In other words, a linear regression model is only \textbf{useful for prediction} in cases where the slope parameter is \textit{non-zero}, or \(\beta \ne 0\). The \textit{parametric} \textbf{Test for Beta} assesses whether data from a sample provide evidence to suggest that this is the case. The test statistic below follows a \(t\)-distribution with \(n-2\) degrees of freedom, and in this course the test will always be \textit{two-tailed} with hypotheses \(\text{H}_0: \beta=0\) and \(\text{H}_1: \beta\ne 0\).

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.33\textwidth}
  \centering
\begin{centering}
\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Test Statistic,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=5cm,height=2.4cm]
\vspace{-0.3cm}
\[t_{n-2}=\dfrac{b\sqrt{S_{xx}}}{s}\]
\end{tcolorbox}
\end{centering}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.66\textwidth}
  \centering
\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Conditions,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=12cm,height=2.4cm]
\begin{itemize}
    \item $\varepsilon_i$ are independent and identically distributed as $\text{N}(0,\sigma^2)$.
    \item The data was obtained through random sampling.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} A bivariate sample has summary statistics:

\vspace{-0.15cm}

\begin{center}
  \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
      $S_{xx}=157.875$ & \qquad & $S_{yy}=188$ & \qquad & $S_{xy}=-133.5$ & \qquad & $n=8$
  \end{tabular}
\end{center}

\vspace{-0.15cm}

Test at the 5\% significance level whether a linear regression model would be useful for prediction.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}\\

\vspace{-0.25cm}

\begin{math}
\begin{array}{ll}
      H_0: & \beta=0\\
      H_1: & \beta \ne 0
    \end{array}
\left]
    \begin{array}{l}
      \alpha=0.05\\
      \textnormal{Two-tailed}
    \end{array}
  \right.
\end{math}

\vspace{0.3cm}

$b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}}=\dfrac{-133.5}{157.875}=-0.8456$\\[0.5em]

$\text{\text{SSR}}=S_{yy}-\dfrac{(S_{xy})^2}{S_{xx}}=188-\dfrac{(-133.5)^2}{157.875}=75.11, \quad s=\sqrt{\dfrac{\text{\text{SSR}}}{n-2}}=\sqrt{\dfrac{75.11}{8-2}}=3.538$\\[1.4em]

Test statistic: $t=\dfrac{b\sqrt{S_{xx}}}{s}=\dfrac{-0.8456\times \sqrt{157.875}}{3.538}=-3.003$

\hspace{11.5cm}
\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay]
\begin{axis}[
  no markers, 
  domain=-3:3, 
  samples=100,
  ymin=0,
  axis lines*=left, 
  every axis y label/.style={at=(current axis.above origin),anchor=south},
  every axis x label/.style={at=(current axis.right of origin),anchor=west},
  height=3cm, 
  width=6.5cm,
  xtick=\empty, 
  ytick=\empty,
  enlargelimits=false, 
  clip=false, 
  axis on top,
  grid = major,
  hide y axis
  ]
\addplot [draw=none, fill=black!70, domain=-3:-1.96] {gauss(x, 0, 1)} \closedcycle;
\addplot [draw=none, fill=black!70, domain=1.96:3] {gauss(x, 0, 1)} \closedcycle;
 \addplot [semithick] {gauss(x, 0, 1)};
\pgfmathsetmacro\valueA{gauss(-1.96,0,1)}
\pgfmathsetmacro\valueB{gauss(0,0,1)}
\draw [semithick, black]  (axis cs:-1.96,0) -- (axis cs:-1.96,\valueA);
\draw [semithick, black]  (axis cs:1.96,0) -- (axis cs:1.96,\valueA);
\node[below] at (axis cs: 0,0)  {$0$};
\node[above] at (axis cs: 0,\valueB)  {$t_6$};
\node[below] at (axis cs: -1.9, 0) {$-2.447$};
\node[above] at (axis cs: -2.4,\valueA) {2.5\%};
\node[below] at (axis cs: 1.9, 0) {$2.447$};
\node[above] at (axis cs: 2.4,\valueA) {2.5\%};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}\\

\vspace{-0.3cm}

Critical value: $t_{6,0.975}=-2.447$\\

Since $-3.003<-2.447$, reject $\text{H}_0$ at the 5\% level of significance. There is  evidence to suggest that the slope parameter is not zero, so evidence that a linear model would be useful for prediction.

\end{tcolorbox}

\pagebreak

\phantomsection\label{ex184q}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex184a]{Exercise 18.4}}{Exercise 18.4}}\label{exercise-18.4}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex184a]{Exercise 18.4}}

\begin{enumerate}
        \item A random sample of bivariate data, sample size 27, yields the following summary statistics:
        \begin{center}
            \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
                $S_{xx}=87.8$ & \qquad & $S_{yy}=415.5$ & \qquad & $S_{xy}=-129.6$ & \qquad & $\text{SSR}=224.2$ 
            \end{tabular}
        \end{center}
        A test for $\beta$  is to be performed on the data.
        \begin{enumerate}
        \item State suitable null and alternative hypotheses.
        \item Show the calculations required to reach a test statistic of $-$4.618.
        \item State the degrees of freedom, $\nu$, of the $t$-distribution required for the test.
        \item Conclude the test at the 5\% level of significance.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item The top speed reached (in mph) and the lap times (in seconds) are recorded for a sample of drivers on a race track. Computer output related to a linear model constructed using this data is below.\\[0.5em]
    \begin{tabular}{lllllll}
        \texttt{y on x regression line} & \qquad &\texttt{n} & \qquad &\texttt{s} & \qquad & \texttt{sum of squares for x}\\
        \texttt{top\_speed = 269.2 - 1.72 $\times$ lap\_time} && \texttt{34} && \texttt{8.49} && \texttt{638.6}
    \end{tabular}\\[0.5em]
    Perform a test for the slope parameter at the 10\% level of significance to assess whether there is evidence that a linear model is useful for prediction for this data.
    \item A nutritionist purchases a sample of drinks sold from vending machines inside a selection of gyms and health centres in his area. The protein content in grams and the sugar content in grams is recorded for each drink. \textbf{Figure 1} shows a scatterplot of the data and \textbf{Output 1} shows results of a hypothesis test conducted (where $\alpha=0.05$) to assess whether a linear model would be useful for prediction. 
    \begin{multicols}{2}
        \begin{center}
        \textbf{Figure 1}\\
            \begin{tikzpicture}
                \draw (-0.3,-0.3) rectangle (5.3,4);
                \foreach \x in {0,5,...,20}{
                \draw (0.25*\x,-0.4) -- (0.25*\x,-0.3) node[below] {\x};
                }
                \node[below] at (2.5,-0.8) {protein (g)};
                \foreach \y in {0,30,60,90}{
                \draw (-0.4,0.04*\y) -- (-0.3,0.04*\y) node[left] {\y};
                }
                \node[above,rotate=90] at (-1,2) {sugar (g)};
                \draw[fill] (2.16249404,0.76998725) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (4.24356756,2.53736405) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.71081735,2.02653705) circle (0.025);

            \end{tikzpicture}
        \end{center}
        \begin{center}
            \textbf{Output 1}\\[1em]
            \RaggedRight
            \texttt{model: sugar = 4.01 - 0.124 $\times$ protein}\\[1em]
            \texttt{t = -0.42295}\\[0.2em]
            \texttt{df = 38}\\[0.2em]
            \texttt{p-value = 0.6747}\\[0.2em]
            \texttt{alternative: true slope parameter is not equal to zero}
        \end{center}
    \end{multicols}
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item State suitable null and alternative hypotheses for the test conducted.
        \item State the sample size.
        \item Determine the critical value required, and hence write a conclusion for the hypothesis test.
        \item State the required assumption about the population of errors for this test.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item A sample of bivariate data with summary statistics below is proposed to be used for a linear model:
        \begin{center}
            \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
                $S_{xx}=75.6$ & \qquad & $S_{xy}=-235.2$ & \qquad & $s^2=4.04$ & \qquad & $n=70$
            \end{tabular}
        \end{center}
        Conduct a parametric test at the 1\% level of significance to assess whether there is evidence that the slope parameter is non-zero.
     \item Test whether the sample data below provides evidence that a linear model would be useful for prediction.
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
            \hline
            $x$ & 38 & 27 & 19 & 45 & 8 & 23 & 11 & 34\\
            \hline
            $y$ & 34 & 53 & 42 & 13 & 19 & 30 & 29 & 45\\
            \hline
        \end{tabular}
    \end{center}
\end{enumerate}

\pagebreak

\section{\texorpdfstring{Test for the Correlation Coefficient \(\rho\)}{Test for the Correlation Coefficient \textbackslash rho}}\label{test-for-the-correlation-coefficient-rho}

The previously introduced correlation coefficient, \(r\), is a sample statistic which measures the strength of the linear association between two variables. It is an estimate of the population parameter \(\rho\) (\textit{``rho''}), where \(-1\leqslant \rho \leqslant 1\). If \(\rho=0\) then there is no \textit{linear} relationship between the variables. Given a sample of bivariate data for which there exists \textit{no} linear relationship, it is regardless common for the sample to give a \textit{non-zero} value for \(r\):

\vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (5,0) node[right] {$x$};
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,3) node[above] {$y$};
            \node at (6.4,2.5) {Population:};
            \node at (6.4,2) {$\rho=0$};
            \node at (6.4,1.2) {\textbf{Sample}:};
            \node at (6.4,0.7) {$r=0.639$};
            \draw[-stealth] (10,0) -- (15,0) node[right] {$x$};
            \draw[-stealth] (10,0) -- (10,3) node[above] {$y$};
            \node at (16.4,2.5) {Population:};
            \node at (16.4,2) {$\rho=0.791$};
            \node at (16.4,1.2) {\textbf{Sample}:};

\end{center}

\vspace{-0.3cm}

The \textit{parametric} \textbf{Test for Rho} assesses a sample of bivariate data for evidence of a \textbf{linear association} between the variables, or \(\rho\ne 0\). The test statistic below follows a \(t_{n-2}\) distribution, and in this course the test will always be \textit{two-tailed} with hypotheses \(\text{H}_0:\rho=0\) and \(\text{H}_1:\rho\ne 0\).

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.33\textwidth}
  \centering
\begin{centering}
\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Test Statistic,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=5cm,height=2.6cm]
\vspace{-0.3cm}
\[t_{n-2}=\dfrac{r\sqrt{n-2}}{\sqrt{1-r^2}}\]
\end{tcolorbox}
\end{centering}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.66\textwidth}
  \centering
\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Conditions,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=12cm,height=2.6cm]
\begin{itemize}
\setlength\itemsep{-0.3em}
    \item The variables are independent and follow an approximately \newline bivariate normal distribution.
    \item The data was obtained through random sampling.
\end{itemize}
\end{tcolorbox}
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

Any conclusion should comment on the evidence for a linear association between the two variables.

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} A bivariate sample has summary statistics:

\vspace{-0.1cm}

\begin{center}
  \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
      $S_{xx}=157.875$ & \qquad & $S_{yy}=188$ & \qquad & $S_{xy}=-133.5$ & \qquad & $n=8$
  \end{tabular}
\end{center}

\vspace{-0.1cm}

Test at the 5\% significance level for evidence of a linear association between $x$ and $y$.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}\\[0.1em]

\begin{math}
\begin{array}{ll}
      H_0: & \rho=0\\
      H_1: & \rho \ne 0
    \end{array}
\left]
    \begin{array}{l}
      \alpha=0.05\\
      \textnormal{Two-tailed}
    \end{array}
  \right.
\end{math}

\vspace{0.3cm}

$r=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{\sqrt{S_{xx}S_{yy}}}=\dfrac{-133.5}{\sqrt{157.875\times 188}}=-0.7749$\\[1em]

Test statistic: $t=\dfrac{r\sqrt{n-2}}{\sqrt{1-r^2}}=\dfrac{-0.7749\sqrt{8-2}}{\sqrt{1-(-0.7749)^2}}=-3.003$
\hspace{1cm}
\begin{tikzpicture}[overlay]
\begin{axis}[
  no markers, 
  domain=-3:3, 
  samples=100,
  ymin=0,
  axis lines*=left, 
  every axis y label/.style={at=(current axis.above origin),anchor=south},
  every axis x label/.style={at=(current axis.right of origin),anchor=west},
  height=3cm, 
  width=6.5cm,
  xtick=\empty, 
  ytick=\empty,
  enlargelimits=false, 
  clip=false, 
  axis on top,
  grid = major,
  hide y axis
  ]
\addplot [draw=none, fill=black!70, domain=-3:-1.96] {gauss(x, 0, 1)} \closedcycle;
\addplot [draw=none, fill=black!70, domain=1.96:3] {gauss(x, 0, 1)} \closedcycle;
 \addplot [semithick] {gauss(x, 0, 1)};
\pgfmathsetmacro\valueA{gauss(-1.96,0,1)}
\pgfmathsetmacro\valueB{gauss(0,0,1)}
\draw [semithick, black]  (axis cs:-1.96,0) -- (axis cs:-1.96,\valueA);
\draw [semithick, black]  (axis cs:1.96,0) -- (axis cs:1.96,\valueA);
\node[below] at (axis cs: 0,0)  {$0$};
\node[above] at (axis cs: 0,\valueB)  {$t_6$};
\node[below] at (axis cs: -1.9, 0) {$-2.447$};
\node[above] at (axis cs: -2.4,\valueA) {2.5\%};
\node[below] at (axis cs: 1.9, 0) {$2.447$};
\node[above] at (axis cs: 2.4,\valueA) {2.5\%};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}\\

Critical value: $t_{6,0.975}=-2.447$\\

Since $-3.003>-2.447$, reject $\text{H}_0$ at the 5\% level of significance.\newline There is evidence to suggest that there is a linear association between the two variables.

\end{tcolorbox}

\pagebreak

\phantomsection\label{ex185q}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex185a]{Exercise 18.5}}{Exercise 18.5}}\label{exercise-18.5}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex185a]{Exercise 18.5}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item A random sample of bivariate data, sample size 27, yields a sample correlation coefficient of $-$0.678. A test for linear association is to be performed on the data.
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item State suitable null and alternative hypotheses.
        \item Show the calculations required to reach a test statistic of $-$4.612.
        \item State the degrees of freedom, $\nu$, of the $t$-distribution required for the test.
        \item Conclude the test at the 5\% level of significance.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item A bivariate random sample has summary statistics:
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
            $S_{xx}=28.92$ & \quad & $S_{yy}=495.0$ & \quad & $S_{xy}=105.3$ & \quad & $n=14$
        \end{tabular}
    \end{center}
    Perform a parametric test to assess at the 10\% level of significance whether there is evidence to suggest a linear association exists between $x$ and $y$.
    \item A guitarist records over the course of several years the price of sets of strings that she buys and the number of days that they last without a string breaking. A scatterplot of the data collected is shown below alongside some summary statistics from computer software.
    \begin{multicols}{2}
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw (0,0) rectangle (5,3.5);
        \node[above] at (2.5,3.5) {scatterplot of price vs days lasted};

        \draw (0.5*\x-3.5,-0.1) -- (0.5*\x-3.5,0) node[below] {\x};
        }
        \node[below] at (2.5,-0.5) {price (£)};
        \foreach \y in {0,30,...,120}{
        \draw (-0.1,0.025*\y) -- (0,0.025*\y) node[left] {\y};
        }
        \node[above,rotate=90] at (-1,1.75) {days lasted};
            \draw[fill] (3.620632,2.42879425) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.1599475,2.17301825) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.251858,2.3056665) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.261534,0.53321475) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.257877,1.46449) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.874575,1.998568) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.941508,2.35303725) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.2640525,1.307669) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.2003315,1.4427435) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (4.098323,1.40786875) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.4929035,3.0956315) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.3260935,1.69551225) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.327406,1.99964525) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.893828,1.58491525) circle (0.04);
        \end{tikzpicture}
    \end{center}

    \texttt{data:}\\[0.1em]
    \texttt{guitar\_strings}

    \vspace{0.4cm}
    
    \texttt{sample correlation coefficient:}\\[0.1em]
    \texttt{0.437}

    \vspace{0.4cm}

    \texttt{sample size:}\\[0.1em]
    \texttt{14}
    
    \vfill\null
    \end{multicols}
    
    \vspace{-0.4cm}
    Test at the 1\% level of significance whether her data provides evidence of a linear association between the price of a set of strings and the time until a string breaks.
    \item \textit{Electrolyte powders} are often used to help improve hydration during exercise. A keen cyclist records the time ($y$ minutes) it takes them to complete a familiar route during a series of rides over the summer, randomly choosing the mass ($x$ grams) of electrolyte powder to add to their water bottle each time. 
    \begin{multicols}{2}
    Summary statistics for the rides are below:
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tabular}{ccccccc}
            $S_{xx}$ & $\quad$ & $S_{yy}$ & $\quad$ & $S_{xy}$ & $\quad$ & $n$\\[0.4em]
            665 & & 567.2 && -346.4 && 20
        \end{tabular}
    \end{center}
    Test whether the data supports a claim from the cyclist that a linear association exists between the amount of electrolyte powder they use and their cycling performance, stating one assumption required.
    \vfill\null
    \columnbreak
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.8]
        \draw (0,-0.5) rectangle (5.5,4.5);
        \foreach \x in {0,5,...,20}{
        \draw (0.25*\x,-0.1-0.5) -- (0.25*\x,0-0.5) node[below] {\x};
        }
        \node[below] at (2.75,-1.1) {electrolytes (g)};
        \foreach \y in {50,55,...,70}{
        \draw (-0.1,0.2*\y-10) -- (0,0.2*\y-10) node[left] {\y};
        }
        \node[above,rotate=90] at (-1.1,2) {time (mins)};
            \draw[fill] (0.25,2.262406) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (0.5,1.744682) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (0.75,1.616336) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (1,3.958988) circle (0.06);

    \end{multicols}
    \item Test whether the sample data below provides evidence of a linear association between $x$ and $y$.
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
            \hline
            $x$ & 38 & 27 & 19 & 45 & 8 & 23 & 11 & 34\\

            \hline
        \end{tabular}
    \end{center}
\end{enumerate}

\pagebreak

\section{\texorpdfstring{Regression Line of \(x\) on \(y\)}{Regression Line of x on y}}\label{regression-line-of-x-on-y}

Each least squares regression line met so far has been an \(y\) \emph{on} \(x\) regression line, calculated with the aim of minimising errors when \emph{predicting} \(y\) \emph{values}. However, a \(y\) \emph{on} \(x\) regression line model cannot be used to predict \(x\) values.

If the prediction of \(x\) values based on \(y\) values is of interest, it is necessary to obtain the equation of an \(x\) \emph{on} \(y\) regression line. This takes a different form and different values of \(a\) and \(b\) as shown below:

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Calculating an $x$ on $y$ Regression Line,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=11cm]
$\hat{X}=a+by \qquad \text{where} \qquad b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{yy}} \qquad \text{and} \qquad a=\bar{x}-b\bar{y}$
\end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{-0.1cm}

\begin{multicols}{2}
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \draw[white] (-1,-1) rectangle (6,5);
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,4) node[above] {$y$};
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (5,0) node[right] {$x$};
            \node at (2.5,-0.5) {Prediction with a $y$ on $x$ model};
            \draw[fill] (1.8717487,1.7974477) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.7352247,2.0554359) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.0775949,3.3894574) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.7449302,1.0352874) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.7070811,2.7362257) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (0.7975562,1.0011431) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.0048085,2.861815) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (0.9914708,1.4786031) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.0668544,3.0221905) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.3598348,3.1216317) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (3.358295,3.327456) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (0.910028,0.9257098) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.0456805,2.3489078) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (0.948168,1.3693633) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.5736475,2.0883494) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.3927657,1.3703404) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (1.5466501,1.6830586) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.3668546,2.4227856) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.7736595,2.6725544) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.0828944,1.8844629) circle (0.05);
        \draw[very thick] (0.5,0.5*0.82+0.43) -- (4,4*0.82+0.43) node[right] {$y$ on $x$};
        \draw[very thin,dashed,black!30] (0.5*0.9+0.16,0.5) -- (4*0.9+0.16,4) node[above] {$x$ on $y$};
        \draw[-Latex,dashed] (3.25,0) -- (3.25,3.25*0.82+0.43) -- (0,3.25*0.82+0.43);
        \end{tikzpicture}
    \end{center}
    \begin{center}
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \draw[white] (-1,-1) rectangle (6,5);
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,4) node[above] {$y$};
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (5,0) node[right] {$x$};
            \node at (2.5,-0.5) {Prediction with an $x$ on $y$ model};
            \draw[fill] (1.8717487,1.7974477) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.7352247,2.0554359) circle (0.05);
\draw[fill] (2.0775949,3.3894574) circle (0.05);

        \draw[very thick] (0.5*0.9+0.16,0.5) -- (4*0.9+0.16,4) node[above] {$x$ on $y$};
        \draw[-Latex,dashed] (0,3.25) -- (3.25*0.9+0.16,3.25) -- (3.25*0.9+0.16,0);
        \end{tikzpicture}
    \end{center}
\end{multicols}

\vspace{-0.3cm}

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} 

Students sitting a French exam are given a mark for each of the two components: speaking ($x$) and listening ($y$). From a random sample of 12 students, the following summary statistics are obtained:

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{ccccc}
        $\Sigma x=157$ & $\Sigma x^2 =2389$ & $\Sigma y=149$ & $\Sigma y^2=1983$ & $\Sigma xy=2143$\\[1em]
        $S_{xx}=334.9$ & $S_{yy}=132.9$ & $S_{xy}=193.6$ & $n=12$ & $r=0.9177$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

Determine the equation for the least squares regression line of $x$ on $y$, and hence predict the speaking mark for a student who has only completed the listening component and obtained a mark of 17 in it.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}\\

$b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{yy}}=\dfrac{193.6}{132.9}=1.457$\\[0.1em]

$a=\bar{x}-b\bar{y}=\dfrac{157}{12}-1.457\times \dfrac{149}{12}=-5.008$\\[0.1em]

$\hat{X}=-5.008+1.457y$\\[0.5em]

For a student with a listening mark ($y$) of 17:\\[0.1em]

$\hat{X}=-5.008+1.457\times 17=15.39$

\end{tcolorbox}

\pagebreak

\pagebreak

\section{Residual Plots}\label{residual-plots}

When considering the use of a linear regression model for bivariate data, it is recommended to produce a plot of \emph{residuals} (\(\varepsilon_i\)) against \emph{fitted values} (\(\hat{Y_i}\)), which have been calculated using the sample regression line. The purpose of this is both to ascertain whether a linear regression model is \emph{the most appropriate} model, as well as to judge the validity of several commonly required assumptions, including those required to obtain the estimates for \(\alpha\) and \(\beta\):

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{ccc}
        $\text{E}(\varepsilon_i)=0$ & $\quad$ \text{and} $\quad$ & $\text{V}(\varepsilon_i)=\sigma^2$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

Below is an illustration of a standard scatterplot showing a sample of bivariate data on the left, with a \(y\) on \(x\) regression line included, and a residual plot for the same data on the right. The data points highlighted with larger circles on the \(y\) against \(x\) scatterplot have their residuals similarly highlighted on the residual plot.

\begin{multicols}{2}

\begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.9]

\vspace{-0.5cm}

It should be observed that the residual plot broadly appears to show:

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=Interpreting Residual Plots,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=11cm]
\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{ccc}
        A random scatter of points... & $\qquad$ & \\
        ...centred on zero... & & $\text{E}(\varepsilon_i)=0$\\
        ...with a constant variance. & & $\text{V}(\varepsilon_i)=\sigma^2$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}
\end{tcolorbox}

This means that the residual plot would give no reason to doubt the assumptions made regarding the errors. This \emph{ideal} residual plot would suggest that the linear regression model is \emph{appropriate}.

The following residual plots are accompanied with relevant comments and suitable conclusions.

The residual plot appears to show a random scatter of points centred on zero, with a constant variance as the fitted value increases. 

\vspace{-1cm}
\begin{center}
    \begin{align*}
        \text{E}(\varepsilon_i)&=0\\
        \text{V}(\varepsilon_i)&=\sigma^2
    \end{align*}
\end{center}

    \node at (6,-2) {Fitted value};
    \node[rotate=90] at (2.2,0) {Residual};
    \node[left] at (3,0) {$0$};
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.56\textwidth}
The residual plot appears to show a `funnel' shaped pattern of points centred on zero, with variance increasing as the fitted value increases. 

\vspace{-1cm}
\begin{center}
    \begin{align*}
        \text{E}(\varepsilon_i)&=0\\
        \text{V}(\varepsilon_i)&\ne\sigma^2
    \end{align*}
\end{center}

This suggests the that linear model is \textbf{not appropriate}.
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.56\textwidth}
The residual plot appears to show a pattern of points following a curve, with constant variance.

\vspace{-1cm}
\begin{center}
    \begin{align*}
        \text{E}(\varepsilon_i)&\ne0\\
        \text{V}(\varepsilon_i)&=\sigma^2
    \end{align*}
\end{center}

This suggests that the linear model is \textbf{not appropriate}.
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

\begin{minipage}[c]{.56\textwidth}
The residual plot appears to show a pattern of points following a curve, with variance decreasing as the fitted value increases.

\vspace{-1cm}
\begin{center}
    \begin{align*}
        \text{E}(\varepsilon_i)&\ne0\\
        \text{V}(\varepsilon_i)&\ne \sigma^2
    \end{align*}
\end{center}

This suggests that the linear model is \textbf{not appropriate}.

\vspace{-0.3cm}

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!,colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} A 1920s study on automobile safety involved recording the stopping distances (in feet) from selected speeds (in miles per hour) for a random sample of 47 cars. Below is a scatterplot of the results with a regression line obtained from a linear model, and a residual plot.

        \foreach \x in {0,5,...,25}{
        \draw (0.2*\x,0) -- (0.2*\x,-0.1);
        \node[below] at (0.2*\x,0) {\x};
        }
        \foreach \y in {0,24,...,96}{
        \node[left] at (0,0.04*\y) {\y};
        \draw (0,0.04*\y) -- (-0.1,0.04*\y);

\end{multicols}

\vspace{-0.5cm}
Use the residual plot to comment on the validity of the linear model.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:} The residual plot appears to show a random scatter of points centred on zero, with a broadly constant variance as the fitted value increases. This suggests the linear model between stopping distance and speed could be appropriate.

\end{tcolorbox}

\begin{enumerate}

\pagebreak

\begin{enumerate}[resume]
    \item Suspension clamps are used to hold electricity cables in place on pylons. Over time these clamps are subject to wear, making them more likely to fail. A random sample of clamps are subject to an increasing force, until they fail. \vspace{-0.3cm}
    Comment on the suitability of the linear model fitted.
    \item A number of randomly selected mobile phones returned as faulty within a six month warranty period each have their time since purchase ($x$) and number of completed battery cycles ($y$) recorded. The equation of the least squares regression line for $y$ on $x$ is obtained, and shown on the scatterplot below alongside a residual plot.
    \begin{multicols}{2}
    \begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw (0,0) -- (6.5,0);
        \foreach \x in {0,1,...,6}{
        \draw (\x,-0.1) -- (\x,0) node[below] {\x};
        }

\pagebreak

\section{Transforming Data}\label{transforming-data}

Where a \textit{non-linear} relationship seems to exist between two variables then it would not be appropriate to use a model which describes a \textit{linear} relationship between them. The presence of a non-linear relationship may be immediately clear from an initial scatterplot, or it may only be revealed once a residual plot for a proposed linear model is created.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.4\textwidth}
\vspace{-0.5cm}

A linear model might initially seem to describe well relationship between $x$ and $y$, as might the result of $R^2=0.9228$.\\

However, the residual plot suggests that a\newline linear model between $x$ and $y$ is \textit{not} appropriate, since the residuals follow a curved pattern.

\end{minipage}%
\begin{minipage}[c]{.6\textwidth}

\end{figure}

\vspace{-0.6cm}

The simulated data above was actually generated using a \textit{quadratic} relationship between \(x\) and \(y\).\newline A scatterplot of \(\sqrt{y}\) against \(x\) for this data is shown below, alongside a residual plot:

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.4\textwidth}
\vspace{-0.5cm}

Both the scatterplot and the residual plot for this new model of $\sqrt{y}$ on $x$ suggest that it is appropriate for the data.\\

This new, better-fitting model also produces an improved value for the coefficient of determination, of $R^2=0.9402$.

\vspace{-0.5cm}

Creating a model such as this, in which one or both variables have some operation performed on them (such as taking the \textit{square root}) is called applying a \textit{transformation} to the data. In practice, knowing which transformation(s) to try will generally be guided by a background understanding of the variables, or previous studies. In this course, any required transformations will be clearly specified.

Familiar statistics may still be calculated using transformed variables:

\begin{center}
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}
    \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
    \hline
        Statistic & For an $y$ on $x$ model & New model & New formula\\
    \hline
    &&&\\[-0.8em]
        Sum of Squares for $x$ & $S_{xx}=\Sigma x^2 - \dfrac{\left(\Sigma x \right)^2}{n}$ & $y$ on $\sqrt{x}$ & $S_{\sqrt{x}\sqrt{x}}=\Sigma (\sqrt{x})^2 - \dfrac{\left(\Sigma \sqrt{x} \right)^2}{n}$\\
        &&&\\[-0.8em]
    \hline
    &&&\\[-0.8em]
        Slope parameter estimate & $b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}}$ & $\log_ay$ on $x$ & $b=\dfrac{S_{x\log_ay}}{S_{xx}}$\\[2ex]
    &&&\\[-0.8em]
    \hline
    &&&\\[-0.8em]
        Correlation coefficient & $r=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{\sqrt{S_{xx}S_{yy}}}$ & $y^3$ on $x$ & $r=\dfrac{S_{xy^3}}{\sqrt{S_{xx}S_{y^3y^3}}}$\\[4ex]
    \hline
    \end{tabular}
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1}
\end{center}

Care should be taken when interpreting these statistics and when calculating using regression line equations.\newline The example opposite illustrates this for the coefficient of determination, and calculating a predicted value.

\pagebreak

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]

\textbf{Problem:} Data was recorded for a sample of 10 cars of their stopping distance ($y$ metres) when travelling at a range of speeds ($x$ miles per hour). The table below shows the results.

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        Speed ($x$) & 16.7 & 21.0 & 26.8 & 29.4 & 34.7 & 42.3 & 51.7 & 58.0 & 65.5 & 73.2\\
        \hline
        Distance ($y$) & 6 & 8 & 13 & 18 & 22 & 38 & 51 & 66 & 82 & 107\\
        \hline
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

The line $\hat{Y}=-32.3+1.75x$ was fitted, and a scatterplot and a residual plot drawn.

\vspace{-0.2cm}

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.6\textwidth}
  \centering
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw[RoyalBlue!1!] (-1,-1) rectangle (7.3,3.3);
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (6.8,0) node[below] {$x$};
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,3.35) node[left] {$y$};
        \node at (3.4,-0.8) {speed};
        \node[rotate=90] at (-1,1.3) {distance};
        \foreach \x in {10,20,...,80}{
        \draw (0.08*\x,0) -- (0.08*\x,-0.1) node[below] {$\scriptstyle{\x}$};
        };
        \foreach \y in {20,40,...,120}{
        \draw (0,0.025*\y) -- (-0.1,0.025*\y) node[left] {$\scriptstyle{\y}$};
        };
        \draw[fill] (1.336,0.15) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.68,0.2) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.144,0.325) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.352,0.45) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.776,0.55) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.384,0.95) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (4.136,1.275) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (4.64,1.65) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (5.24,2.05) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (5.856,2.675) circle (0.04);
        \draw (1.48,0.001875) -- (6,2.47375);
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.4\textwidth}
  \centering
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw[RoyalBlue!1!] (-1.5,-2.5) rectangle (4.5,1.8);
        \draw[thick] (0,-1.5) rectangle (3.6,1.5);
        \draw[dashed] (0,0) -- (3.6,0);
        \node at (1.8,-2.3) {fitted value};
        \node[rotate=90] at (-1,0) {residual};
        \foreach \x in {0,20,...,120}{
        \draw (0.03*\x,-1.6) -- (0.03*\x,-1.5);
        \node[below] at (0.03*\x,-1.5) {$\scriptstyle{\x}$};
        };
        \foreach \y in {-12,-8,...,12}{
        \draw (-0.1,0.1*\y) -- (0,0.1*\y);
        \node[left] at (0,0.1*\y) {$\scriptstyle{\y}$};
        };
        \draw[fill] (0.18,0.90633953) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (0.24,0.35365384) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (0.39,-0.16159663) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (0.54,-0.11670891) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (0.66,-0.64443778) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.14,-0.37476598) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.53,-0.72017191) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.98,-0.32294396) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.46,-0.03576784) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.21,1.11639964) circle (0.04);

    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

\vspace{-0.5cm}

A new linear model was created between speed ($x$) and the square root of the stopping distance ($\sqrt{y}$). For this new model, a scatterplot and residual plot were produced, and a least squares regression line was computed as: \texttt{square root of distance = -0.016 + 0.14 $\times$ speed}

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.6\textwidth}
  \centering
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw[RoyalBlue!1!] (-1,-1) rectangle (7.3,3.3);
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (6.8,0) node[below] {$x$};
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0) -- (0,3) node[left] {$\sqrt{y}$};
        \node at (3.4,-0.8) {speed};
        \node[rotate=90] at (-1,1.3) {$\sqrt{\text{distance}}$};
        \foreach \x in {10,20,...,80}{
        \draw (0.08*\x,0) -- (0.08*\x,-0.1) node[below] {$\scriptstyle{\x}$};
        };
        \foreach \y in {0,2,...,10}{
        \draw (0,0.25*\y) -- (-0.1,0.25*\y) node[left] {$\scriptstyle{\y}$};
        };
    \draw[fill] (1.336,0.6123725) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.68,0.70710675) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.144,0.90138775) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.352,1.06066025) circle (0.04);

\draw[fill] (4.64,2.0310095) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (5.24,2.26384625) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (5.856,2.58602) circle (0.04);
    \draw (0.8,0.346) -- (6.4,2.796);
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.4\textwidth}
  \centering
    \begin{tikzpicture}
        \draw[RoyalBlue!1!] (-1.5,-2.5) rectangle (4.5,1.8);
        \draw[thick] (0,-1.5) rectangle (3.6,1.5);
        \draw[dashed] (0,0) -- (3.6,0);
        \node at (1.8,-2.3) {fitted value};
        \node[rotate=90] at (-1,0) {residual};
        \foreach \x in {0,2,...,12}{
        \draw (0.3*\x,-1.6) -- (0.3*\x,-1.5);
        \node[below] at (0.3*\x,-1.5) {$\scriptstyle{\x}$};
        };
        \foreach \y in {-0.2,-0.1,0,0.1,0.2}{
        \draw (-0.1,5*\y) -- (0,5*\y);
        \node[left] at (0,5*\y) {$\scriptstyle{\y}$};
        };
        \draw[fill] (0.734847,0.618051685) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (0.8485281,-0.502807) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.0816653,-0.684666355) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.2727923,0.67742755) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.4071248,-0.80053236) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.8493242,1.239657335) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.1424284,-0.46739347) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.4372114,0.027531465) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.7166155,-0.57540742) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.103224,0.468138565) circle (0.04);

    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

\vspace{-0.5cm}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item[(a)] Comment on the appropriateness of the linear model of $y$ on $x$.
    \item[(b)] Comment on the appropriateness of the new model of $\sqrt{y}$ on $x$.
    \item[(c)] By first creating a new table for $x$ and $\sqrt{y}$ and finding summary statistics for this data, calculate the coefficient of determination for this new model and interpret its value.
    \item[(d)] Estimate the stopping distance at a speed of 50 miles per hour.
\end{enumerate}

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item[(a)] The residual plot appears to show a curved pattern, so this linear model is not appropriate.
    \item[(b)] The residual plot for the new model appears to show a random scatter of points centered on zero, with a constant variance as fitted values increase, so the new model seems appropriate.
    \item[(c)] $r=\dfrac{S_{x\sqrt{y}}}{\sqrt{S_{xx}S_{\sqrt{y}\sqrt{y}}}}=\dfrac{3400.0}{\sqrt{10758.9\times 5951.5}}=0.9987$ and $R^2=0.99987^2=0.997$.\\[0.5em]
    99.7\% of the variation of $\sqrt{\text{distance}}$ is explained by the model.
    \item[(d)] $\sqrt{\text{distance}}=-0.016+0.14\times 50=6.984\text{, so distance}=6.984^2=48.8\text{ metres}$
\end{enumerate}

\end{tcolorbox}

\end{enumerate}

\pagebreak

\section{Confidence Intervals and Prediction Intervals}\label{confidence-intervals-and-prediction-intervals}

One purpose for adopting a regression approach to examine the relationship between two variables is the wish to make predictions related to the response variable (\(Y\)) when given a particular value for the predictor (\(x\)).

The least squares regression line equation of \(\hat{Y_i}=a+bx_i\) gives an \emph{estimate} for the \textbf{mean} value of the response variable for any given \(x_i\), whilst the \emph{true} mean response value for a given \(x_i\), which is denoted \(\text{E}(Y_i\,|\,x_i)\), is unknown.

It can be desirable to obtain a \textbf{confidence interval} for \(\text{E}(Y_i\,|\,x_i)\).

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.45\textwidth}
    \begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=1.3]
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0.6) -- (5,0.6) node[right] {$x$};
            \draw[-stealth] (0,0.6) -- (0,3.7) node[left] {$y$};
            \fill[black!20] plot[smooth] coordinates {(0.1,1.78) (1,2) (2,2.25) (3,2.57) (4,2.95) (4.8,3.27)} -- (4.8,2.836) plot[smooth] coordinates { (4.8,2.836) (4,2.64) (3,2.38) (2,2.055) (1,1.67) (0.1,1.31) } -- (0.1,1.78);
             \node at (5,3.1) {$\hat{Y}$};
             \node[below] at (0.9,0.6) {$x_i$};
             \draw[ultra thin,dashed] (0.9,0.6) -- (0.9,1.63);
             \draw[fill=black!60,black!60] (0.87,1.965) -- (0.93,1.980) -- (0.93,1.643) -- (0.87,1.619) -- cycle;
             \draw[thick] (0.1,1.51+0.32*0.1) -- (4.8,4.8*0.32+1.51);
             \draw[stealth-] (0.9,2.1) -- (1.1,3) node[above] {$\text{E}(Y_i|x_i)$};

\draw[fill] (2.6186868,2.3546323) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.7584162,2.4601992) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.4879102,2.5396250) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.3974763,2.2783382) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.6557084,2.3733230) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.7062729,2.6201000) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.5296301,1.9896044) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.3368203,1.8191709) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.1984621,1.8486193) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.2013159,2.5334466) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.9583961,2.6880222) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.5899918,2.1198890) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.8050951,3.4171817) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (0.1162737,0.7826276) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.6717217,2.3479134) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.4043482,2.0867250) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (0.6744156,1.9708199) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.8989277,2.5649783) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.7692380,2.8082608) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.4906288,2.6225438) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.8172254,2.4503822) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (4.6524560,3.2796855) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.3943288,3.1373983) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.6075723,1.9088071) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.4742392,1.7769002) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (4.0990849,2.9308218) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.9740217,2.4904545) circle (0.025);

\draw[fill] (3.4771699,2.1337608) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.1449574,3.0095094) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.3392608,2.9804617) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.1770795,2.4956330) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.8333715,2.9312680) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.9179660,2.1203264) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.2977681,1.3818752) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.8452314,2.5099713) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.6552033,2.8847268) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.9040389,2.2179828) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.8515219,2.7642650) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (4.4131350,3.3622880) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.1054952,2.0879375) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.6863044,2.1624035) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.4952754,1.8564243) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.8043018,2.3834828) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.2547655,1.8662932) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.0222749,0.9866091) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.7958871,2.6586097) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.6471418,2.2065016) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.3868165,2.9926895) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.2517952,1.8377142) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.4622542,2.2122443) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.8386178,2.5208674) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.4068734,1.5852268) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.0176214,1.6202520) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.0523203,1.4718609) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.8703864,2.1969153) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.9254964,2.5402209) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.8520711,2.1735303) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.6727187,2.6713170) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.6747436,2.6221642) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (2.2863092,2.8204645) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.5728943,1.9649286) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.4744290,2.3868745) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.1110311,2.3838065) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (3.1142563,2.4495563) circle (0.025);
\draw[fill] (1.4003549,1.6048878) circle (0.025);
        \end{tikzpicture}
        \end{center}
\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.55\textwidth}
It can be shown that the \textit{point estimate} for the mean response, $\hat{Y_i}$, has a sample standard error of:

$$s\sqrt{\dfrac{1}{n}+\dfrac{(x_i-\bar{x})^2}{S_{xx}}}$$

If the errors, $\varepsilon_i$, are identically and independently distributed as $\text{N}\left(0,\sigma^2\right)$ then the formula below, given on Page 5 of the Data Booklet, can be derived.
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=A Confidence Interval for the Mean Response,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=15.5cm]
A $100(1-\alpha)\%$ \text{CI} for $\text{E}(Y_i\,|\,x_i)$ is given by: $\hat{Y_i}\,\pm\,t_{n-2,1-\alpha/2}\times s\sqrt{\dfrac{1}{n}+\dfrac{(x_i-\bar{x})^2}{S_{xx}}}$
\end{tcolorbox}

With all confidence intervals, the level of confidence relates to the success rate of the \emph{procedure used to generate the interval}, rather than the probability of a calculated interval containing the mean response value \(\text{E}(Y_i\,|\,x_i)\).

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} 

The lengths in centimetres ($x$) and masses in kilograms ($y$) of 12 salmon were measured, with the results:

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{ccccc}
        $\Sigma x=30$ & $\Sigma y=540$ & $S_{xx}=12$ & $S_{yy}=4800$ & $S_{xy}=150$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

Construct a 95\% confidence interval for the mean length of salmon with mass 3kg.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}\\

$b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}}=\dfrac{150}{12}=12.5, \quad a=\bar{y}-b\bar{x}=\dfrac{540}{12}-12.5\times \dfrac{30}{12}=13.75 \quad \implies \quad \hat{Y_i}=13.75+12.5x_i$\\[0.3em]

$\hat{Y}=13.75+12.5\times 3=51.75$\\[0.3em]

$\text{\text{SSR}}=S_{yy}-\dfrac{(S_{xy})^2}{S_{xx}}=4800-\dfrac{150^2}{12}=2925, \quad s^2=\dfrac{\text{\text{SSR}}}{n-2}=\dfrac{2925}{10}=292.5, \quad s=\sqrt{292.5}=17.1$\\[0.3em]

A 95\% CI for $\text{E}(Y\,|\,x=3)$ is $51.75\,\pm\,2.228 \times 17.1\times \sqrt{\dfrac{1}{12}+\dfrac{(3-2.5)^2}{12}}=51.75\,\pm\,12.30$\\[0.3em]

So a 95\% confidence interval for the mean length of a 3kg salmon is $(39.45\text{cm}\,,\,64.05\text{cm})$.

\end{tcolorbox}

\pagebreak

Instead of an interval for the value of \textit{mean} response, it may be desired to obtain an interval for \textbf{individual} response values for a given \(x_i\), denoted as \(Y_i\,|\,x_i\).

The desired interval is called a \textbf{prediction interval}.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering

\end{minipage}%
  \centering
\begin{minipage}[c]{.4\textwidth}

Since the value of a randomly sampled individual can be expected to have more variability than the value of a sample mean, a wider interval around $\hat{Y_i}$ is needed.\\[0.5em]

Note the more darkly shaded region lying closer to the regression line, showing the narrower confidence interval for the same data.\\[0.5em]

In the long-run, a 95\% prediction interval for $Y_i\,|\,x_i$, such as indicated in the diagram, will tend to capture 95\% of all observed response values for any given $x_i$.

\end{minipage}
\end{figure}

\begin{tcolorbox}[halign title=center,title=A Prediction Interval for an Individual Response,center,colback=red!5,colframe=red!70!black,width=15cm]
A $100(1-\alpha)\%$ \text{PI} for $Y_i\,|\,x_i$ is given by: $\hat{Y_i}\,\pm\,t_{n-2,1-\alpha/2}\times s\sqrt{1+\dfrac{1}{n}+\dfrac{(x_i-\bar{x})^2}{S_{xx}}}$
\end{tcolorbox}

This formula, also given on Page 5 of the Data Booklet, relies also on the errors, \(\varepsilon_i\), being identically and independently distributed as \(\text{N}\left(0,\sigma^2\right)\)

\begin{tcolorbox}[title=Example,colback=RoyalBlue!1!, colframe=RoyalBlue]
\textbf{Problem:} 

The lengths in centimetres ($x$) and masses in kilograms ($y$) of 12 salmon were measured, with the results:

\begin{center}
    \begin{tabular}{ccccc}
        $\Sigma x=30$ & $\Sigma y=540$ & $S_{xx}=12$ & $S_{yy}=4800$ & $S_{xy}=150$
    \end{tabular}
\end{center}

Construct a 95\% prediction interval for the length of salmon with mass 3kg.

\tcblower
\textbf{Solution:}\\

$b=\dfrac{S_{xy}}{S_{xx}}=\dfrac{150}{12}=12.5, \quad a=\bar{y}-b\bar{x}=\dfrac{540}{12}-12.5\times \dfrac{30}{12}=13.75 \quad \implies \quad \hat{Y_i}=13.75+12.5x_i$\\[0.5em]

$\hat{Y}=13.75+12.5\times 3=51.75$\\[0.5em]

$\text{\text{SSR}}=S_{yy}-\dfrac{(S_{xy})^2}{S_{xx}}=4800-\dfrac{150^2}{12}=2925, \quad s^2=\dfrac{\text{\text{SSR}}}{n-2}=\dfrac{2925}{10}=292.5, \quad s=\sqrt{292.5}=17.1$\\[0.5em]

A 95\% PI for $Y\,|\,x=3$ is $51.75\,\pm\,2.228 \times 17.1\times \sqrt{1+\dfrac{1}{12}+\dfrac{(3-2.5)^2}{12}}=51.75\,\pm\,40.03$\\[0.5em]

So a 95\% prediction interval for the observed length of a 3kg salmon is $(11.72\text{cm}\,,\,91.78\text{cm})$.

\end{tcolorbox}

\pagebreak

\phantomsection\label{ex188q}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex189a]{Exercise 18.9}}{Exercise 18.9}}\label{exercise-18.9}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex189a]{Exercise 18.9}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Students sitting a French exam are given a mark for each of the two components: speaking ($x$) and listening ($y$). Data is gathered from a random sample of 12 students, and a regression line obtained.
    \begin{multicols}{2}
    Summary statistics are calculated as:
    \begin{align*}
        \Sigma x &= 157\\[0.5em]
        S_{xx} &= 334.9\\[0.5em]
        s &= 1.45\\[0.5em]
        n&=12
    \end{align*}
    \vfill\null 
\begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.6]
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0.8) -- (5.2,0.8) node[right] {$x$};
        \draw[-stealth] (0,0.8) -- (0,5.25) node[above] {$y$}; 
        \node at (2.5,-0.6) {speaking};
        \node[above,rotate=90] at (-1.5,2.5) {listening};
        \foreach \x in {5,10,...,20}{
        \draw (0.25*\x,0.8) -- (0.25*\x,0.7) node[below] {\x};
        }
        \foreach \y in {5,10,...,20}{
        \draw (0,0.25*\y) -- (-0.1,0.25*\y) node[left] {\y};
        }
        \draw[thick] (0.25*3,0.25*3*0.578+0.25*04.854) -- (0.25*20,0.25*20*0.578+0.25*4.854) node[right] {$\hat{Y}=4.854+0.578x$};
\draw[fill] (1.75,2.25) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (5,3.25) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (3,3.25) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (4.75,4.25) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (1.5,2.25) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (4.25,3.5) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (4.25,4) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (4.5,4) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (3,3) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (1,1.5) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (4,3.75) circle (0.06);
\draw[fill] (2.25,2.25) circle (0.06);
    \end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}
    \end{multicols}
    \vspace{-0.6cm}
    A pupil achieves a speaking score of 15. They are yet to complete the listening component.
    \begin{enumerate}
    \item Obtain a 95\% confidence interval for the mean listening score given a speaking score of 15.
    \item Obtain a 95\% prediction interval for the listening score given a speaking score of 15.
\end{enumerate}
    \item 26 randomly selected trees in a forest have their diameter at a set distance from the ground measured, in inches, and the height of each tree also measured, in feet. The equation of the least squares regression line for $x$ on $y$ is obtained, and shown on the scatterplot below.
\vspace{-0.2cm}
    \begin{center}
    \begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.9]
        \draw (0,0) -- (6.2,0);
        \foreach \x in {0,1,...,6}{
        \draw (\x,-0.1) -- (\x,0) node[below] {\x};
        }
        \node at (3.1,-1) {Tree diameter (inches)};
        \draw (0,0) -- (0,3.5);
        \foreach \y in {40,50,...,70}{
        \draw (-0.1,0.1*\y-4) -- (0,0.1*\y-4) node[left] {\y};
        }
        \node[rotate=90] at (-1,1.5) {Tree height (feet)};
        \draw[fill] (1.9105299,0.770579) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (1.3785325,0.69114) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (3.2509885,1.498287) circle (0.04);
\draw[fill] (2.6561971,1.476465) circle (0.04);

    \item Biologists studying populations of European hamsters record the tail length ($x$ centimetres) and body mass ($y$ grams) of a random sample of 12 wild hamsters found living in central Vienna.     \vspace{0.2cm}
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Calculate an estimate of the error variance.
        \item Calculate a 95\% prediction interval for the daily new visitors of a site with an annual social media marking spend of £15,000.
        \item Explain why a prediction interval is more likely to be of interest to the magazine's creators than a confidence interval.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
\end{figure}
    \item The Scottish physicist James Forbes was interested in finding a way to estimate altitude from measurements of the boiling temperature of water. Some of his observations on boiling point ($y$, in $F^{\circ}$) and barometric pressure ($x$, in inches of mercury) are summarised below. After plotting a scatterplot for this data, a least squares regression line was obtained with the equation $\hat{Y}=155.29+1.9018x$, from which a residual plot was also created.

\begin{enumerate}[resume]
    \item The website \textit{Fife Walking} contains an extensive collection of walking routes grouped by area. The length ($x$ miles) and total vertical ascent ($y$ metres) is shown in \textbf{table 1} below for a random sample of eleven routes in the \textit{Ochil Hills} area.
    \vspace{-0.4cm}
    \begin{center}
        \textbf{Table 1}\\[0.3em]
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
            \hline
            \textbf{Route Name} & \textbf{Distance (miles)} & \textbf{Ascent (metres)}\\
            \hline
            Beld Hill and Craig Rossie& 6   &460\\\hline
            Dumyat& 3.8 &500\\\hline
            Myretoun Hill and Balquharn Glen&   4   &470\\\hline
            Colsnaur Circuit&   5   &580\\\hline
            Core Hill   &6.5    &480\\\hline
            Eastbow Hills and Steele's Knowe    &6.2    &430\\\hline
            King's Seat Circuit&    5.6&    600\\\hline
            Burn of Sorrow Circular&    6   &700\\\hline
            Hilfoot Hill and Castle Campbell    &3.2&   300\\\hline
            Innerdownie, Whitewisp and Glenquey Circuit&    8&  550\\\hline
            Ben Shee    &5  &365\\
            \hline
        \end{tabular}
    \end{center}
    The data is displayed below in a scatterplot below (\textbf{figure 1}), with the equation of a $y$ on $x$ least squares regression line calculated as $\hat{Y}=304.08+35.25x$.
    \begin{center}
        \textbf{Figure 1}\\[0.3em]
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \draw (0,0) -- (10,0);
            \draw (0,0) -- (0,8);
            \foreach \x in {0,1,...,10}{
            \draw (\x,0) -- (\x,-0.2) node[below] {\x};
            }
            \foreach \y in {0,100,...,800}{
            \draw (0,0.01*\y) -- (-0.2,0.01*\y) node[left] {\y};
            }
            \node at (5,-1.2) {distance (miles)};
            \node[above,rotate=90] at (-1.2,4) {ascent (metres)};
            \draw[fill] (6,4.6) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (3.8,5) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (4,4.7) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (5,5.8) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (6.5,4.8) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (6.2,4.3) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (5.6,6) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (6,7) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (3.2,3) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (8,5.5) circle (0.08);
            \draw[fill] (5,3.65) circle (0.08);
            \draw (2.5,3.92205) -- (9,6.2133) node[right] {$\hat{Y}=304.08+35.25x$};
        \end{tikzpicture}
    \end{center}
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Calculate the correlation coefficient and interpret it, with reference to the context.
        \item Explain why the regression line obtained would not be suitable for estimating the distance a route covers if it is known that the total ascent is 430 metres.
        \item Determine the equation of a more suitable regression line, and use it to estimate the distance covered for a route with an ascent of 430 metres.
        \item A walker completes a route in the Lomond Hills area, which also features on the \textit{Fife Walking} website. Explain why it would may not be appropriate for them to use either of the regression lines obtained to predict their total ascent if their distance covered is known.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\begin{enumerate}

    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Lower fence $=6.5$ and upper fence $=22.5$. Since both 4 and 27 lie outwith this interval, they are outliers.
        \item

    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}

    \item 
    \begin{tikzpicture}[baseline=(current bounding box.north)]

    \draw[fill] (8,0.3) circle (2pt);
    \draw[fill] (4,0.6) circle (2pt);
    \draw[fill] (10,0.3) circle (2pt);
    \draw[fill] (9,0.9) circle (2pt);
    \draw[fill] (6,0.6) circle (2pt);
    \draw[fill] (5,0.3) circle (2pt);
    \end{tikzpicture}
    \item $\text{median}=7.5$ and $\text{interquartile range}=3.25$
    \item $\text{mean}=7.33$ and $\text{sd}=2.35$
    \item Shoe sizes are discrete data.
    \end{multicols}
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item A legend should be provided for the diagram, such as $3|2=3.2\text{ metres}$.
        \item The stem-and-leaf diagram shows that trees in Woodland A were taller, on average.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The fences are $1.13$ and $1.85$. Since both the minimum value of $1.00$ and the maximum of $1.90$ lie outwith this interval, the \textit{iris setosa} data contains at least two outliers.
        \item The median length of \textit{iris versicolor} petals appears to be greater than the length of \textit{iris setosa} petals. The length of \textit{iris versicolor} petals appears to be more varied than those of the \textit{iris setosa} species, as seen by their interquartile ranges of $0.18$ and $0.60$ centimetres respectively.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\endgroup
