\subsection*{Chapter 3 Answers - Sampling Theory}\label{chapter-3-answers---sampling-theory}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Chapter 3 Answers - Sampling Theory}

\begingroup
\footnotesize

\textbf{Note:} Whilst the answers included here are intended to include an appropriate level of detail, variations in the amount and type of information given in exam questions will require a flexible and common-sense approach to be used in any exam. Past exam questions and marking schemes should be used as a guide, as well as course reports and relevant CPD events.

\phantomsection\label{ex31a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex31q]{Exercise 3.1}}{Exercise 3.1}}\label{exercise-3.1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex31q]{Exercise 3.1}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item People who use his local supermarket.
            \item All shoppers who use the self-checkout tills on that Sunday morning.
        \end{enumerate}
        \item The sampling frame excludes people who prefer to shop at other times, such as during the evening or midweek. It also only includes those using self-checkout tills, likely excluding those who have a large trolly of shopping. Both of these issues are likely to make the sample unrepresentative of the target population, and may introduce bias.
        \item The sampling methods is non-random and so will likely introduce bias to the data, making it unsuitable.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item There may be so many flowers that it is too time-consuming and impractical to make a sampling frame comprised of individual flowers.
        \item A better choice of sampling unit for the sampling frame may be square metres of field, forming a numbered grid.
        \item A random number generator will help avoid possible bias in the selection of square metres to include in the sample, by ensuring it is random and not influenced by the botanist. It should help produce a sample that is more representative of the population of wildflowers.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex32a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex32q]{Exercise 3.2}}{Exercise 3.2}}\label{exercise-3.2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex32q]{Exercise 3.2}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item One-stage cluster sampling.
        \item Stratified sampling.
        \item Systematic sampling.
        \item Simple random sampling.
        \item Quota sampling.
        \item Convenience Sampling.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Each of the different types of room may have their own distinct characteristics in terms of ageing or the quality of maintenance. Stratified sampling will ensure that a proportionate number from each type of room will be included in the sample.
        \item 30 out of 360 cabins means 1 in every 12 cabins should be sampled, so the sample should contain: 2 Deluxe cabins; 6 Premium cabins and 22 Standard cabins. To sample the Deluxe cabins, a numbered list of those cabins should be obtained as a suitable sampling frame, and then a random number generator used to produce 2 different random numbers. Those cabins corresponding to the numbers selected should be included in the sample. This process should then be repeated for the Premium cabins and the Standard cabins, using a numbered sampling frame for each and randomly selecting the appropriate number of cabins of each type. Combining all of the selected cabins will make a stratified sample.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Convenience sampling.
        \item He only samples coral reefs close to his town, ignoring that those near other towns or villages, or rural locations, may have a different level of health. This means the sample is unlikely to be representative of the the population of coral reefs along the West Coast of Scotland. (Alternatively, the non-random selection method may be discussed.)
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Using cluster sampling means less physical travelling to a larger number of petrol stations, which would cost more and take more time.
        \item To sample 40 pumps, 5 petrol stations are needed if one-stage cluster sampling is used. A sampling frame of all the petrol stations numbered from 1 to 126 should be drawn up. A random number generator can then be used to select 5 numbers, and the petrol stations corresponding to those numbers should be included in the sample. From each of those petrol stations, all of the pumps should be sampled.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Systematic sampling.
        \item She didn't use a random starting point, so it is not a true random sampling process that is being used.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Quota sampling.
        \item This is a non-random sampling method which is liable to produce a sample not representative of the target population, and may introduce bias into the data gathered. 
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{re3a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[re3q]{Chapter 3 Review Exercise}}{Chapter 3 Review Exercise}}\label{chapter-3-review-exercise}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[re3q]{Chapter 3 Review Exercise}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item Convenience sampling.
            \item This is a non-random sampling method which may produce a sample that is not representative of the target population, and may introduce bias to the data.
        \end{enumerate}
        \item
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item Simple random sampling.
            \item With the 150 employees in an ordered list, the random cell selection tool could be used to select the first employee to be included in the sample. From there, every 5th employee on the list could be selected, looping back around to the start of the list if needed until the sample of size 30 has been obtained.
        \end{enumerate}
        \item
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item A numbered list of the restaurants in the chain could be obtained. A random number generator could be used to pick several numbers, and those restaurants corresponding to the numbers could be selected to be sampled. For a one-stage cluster sample, all employees at those restaurants should be sampled.
            \item One advantage of this method is that it will require less restaurants to be visted for an equivalent sample size, reducing travel costs. One disadvantage is that visiting less restaurants may make it more likely than any restaurants in the chain experiencing problems have none of their employees included in the sample.
        \end{enumerate}
    \end{enumerate}
    \item For an overall sample size of 150 out of 5000 (3\%), the sample needs to include 3 people from Management, 12 from Sales and 135 from the Shop Floor. For each strata, an ordered sampling frame should be obtained, and numbers assigned. For the Management sample, a random number generator should be used to choose 3 numbers, and those staff corresponding to those numbers should be selected to be in the sample. The same process should be repeated to choose 12 from the Sales samling frame, and 135 from the Shop Floor sampling frame. Put together, this will make a sample of size 150 with proportions from each strata matching those of the target population.
    \item The pizzas sampled will only be those from takeaway restaurants, and may not be representative of pizzas sold in the UK as a whole. Additionally, only those takeaways with a presence on social media will be included.
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item It is unlikely to be possible for the researcher to obtain a list of all pupils in Scotland studying Higher Mathematics.
        \item From an ordered list of all centres in Scotland which  presents pupils for Higher Maths, simple random sampling could be used to select a number of schools. For each of those schools selected, an ordered list of all pupils taking Higher Maths should be obtained, and used to selected a random number of pupils from each selected school. Each of those pupils selected should be included in the overall sample.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item A random letter selector and a random number generator could be used together to select a letter and a number, to correspond to grids, such as 'C' and '5' to select grid C5. This process could be repeated to create a list of grids to for the botanist to visit and study the health of flowers in those grids.
\end{enumerate}

\endgroup

\begin{multicols}{3}
\begin{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\text{P}(X)=\tfrac{5}{9}$
        \item $\text{P}(X|Y)=\tfrac{3}{4}$
        \item $\text{P}(X\cap Y)=\tfrac{1}{3}$
        \item $\text{P}(X\cup Y)=\tfrac{2}{3}$
        \item $\text{P}(Y)=\tfrac{4}{9}$
        \item $\text{P}(Y|X)=\tfrac{3}{5}$
        \item $\text{P}(Y\cap X)=\tfrac{1}{3}$
        \item $\text{P}(Y\cup X)=\tfrac{2}{3}$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\text{P}(\text{scored})=\tfrac{73}{135}$
        \item $\text{P}(\text{scored }|\text{ 3-pointer})=\tfrac{15}{41}$
        \item $\text{P}(\text{3-pointer})=\tfrac{41}{135}$
        \item $\text{P}(\text{3-pointer }|\text{ scored})=\tfrac{15}{73}$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item P(rainy)$=0.3$
        \item P(not rainy)$=0.7$
        \item P(leaves | rainy)$=0.4$
        \item P(does not leave | rainy)$=0.6$
        \item P(leaves | not rainy)$=0.9$
        \item P(does not leave | not rainy)$=0.1$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\text{P}(A)=\tfrac{2}{5}$
        \item $\text{P}(B)=\tfrac{2}{5}$
        \item $\text{P}(C)=\tfrac{1}{3}$
        \item $\text{P}(A\cap B)=\tfrac{2}{15}$
        \item $\text{P}(B \cup C)=\tfrac{3}{5}$
        \item $\text{P}(A \cap B \cap C)=\tfrac{1}{15}$
        \item $\text{P}(A | C)=\tfrac{3}{5}$
        \item $\text{P}(C | B)=\tfrac{1}{3}$
        \item $\text{P}(\overline{B} | C)=\tfrac{3}{5}$
        \item $\text{P}(C | \overline{A})=\tfrac{2}{9}$
        \item $\text{P}((A \cap B) | C)=\tfrac{1}{5}$
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}
\end{multicols}

    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\tfrac{2}{3}$
        \item $0.7667$
        \item $0.1$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}

\subsection*{Chapter 13 - Control Charts}\label{chapter-13---control-charts}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Chapter 13 - Control Charts}

\begingroup
\footnotesize

\phantomsection\label{ex131a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex131q]{Exercise 13.1}}{Exercise 13.1}}\label{exercise-13.1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex131q]{Exercise 13.1}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item The process is out of statistical control, since sample 6 falls below the lower control line. The process should have been stopped after sample 6.
    \item The process is out of statistical control, since from samples 4 to 8 at least four of the five samples lie above the upper 1-sigma line. The process should have been stopped after sample 8.
    \item The process is under statistical control.
    \item The process is out of statistical control, since from samples 2 to 9 eight consecutive samples lie above the centre line. The process should have been stopped after sample 9.
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex132a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex132q]{Exercise 13.2}}{Exercise 13.2}}\label{exercise-13.2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex132q]{Exercise 13.2}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item UCL = 82.5, +2$\sigma$ = 81.75, +1$\sigma$ = 80.75, target = 80, -1$\sigma$ = 79.25, -2$\sigma$ = 78.5, LCL = 77.75
        \item UCL = 500.72, +2$\sigma$ = 495.81, +1$\sigma$ = 490.91, target = 486, -1$\sigma$ = 481.09, -2$\sigma$ = 476.19, LCL = 471.28
    \end{enumerate}
    \item $+2\sigma=1.447$, $-2\sigma=1.353$
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $+1\sigma=231.79$, $-1\sigma=228.21$
        \item The process is out of statistical control since, from samples 9 to 11, two of the three samples lie above the upper 2-sigma limit. The process should have been stopped after sample 11.
        \item A required assumption is that the samples of bottles are random.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $144-3\times\dfrac{18}{\sqrt{7}}=123.59$
        \item $130.4\leqslant \bar{x} \leqslant 164.4$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The process is out of statistical control since eight consecutive points lie above the centre line, from samples 9 to 16. However, since these values suggest the lenses being produced have a higher resolution than the target value, which is a good thing, they may not be concerned.
        \item Samples of size 9 are being taken.
        \item Two required assumptions are that:
        \begin{itemize}
            \item The samples of lenses taken are random.
            \item The resolution scores of manufactured lenses are normally distributed.
        \end{itemize}
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item UCL = 121, +2$\sigma$ = 119, +1$\sigma$ = 117, target = 115, -1$\sigma$ = 113, -2$\sigma$ = 111, LCL = 109.\newline
        The process is out of statistical control since, from samples 2 to 4, two out of three sample means lie above the upper 2-sigma limit.
        \item $\bar{\bar{x}}=92$
        \item UCL = 101, LCL = 83 
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{multicols}{2}
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item \phantom{ }\\
                \begingroup
                \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}
                \begin{tabular}{|r|l|}
                \hline
                    Population mean & 72\\
                    \hline
                    Standard deviation & 15\\
                    \hline
                    Sample size & 25\\
                    \hline
                    \hline
                    Upper Control Limit & 81\\
                    \hline
                    $+2\sigma$ Limit & 78\\
                    \hline
                    $+1\sigma$ Limit &75\\
                    \hline
                    Target Value & 72\\
                    \hline
                    $-1\sigma$ Limit & 69\\
                    \hline
                    $-2\sigma$ Limit & 66\\
                    \hline
                    Lower Control Limit & 63\\
                    \hline
                \end{tabular}
                \endgroup
            \item \phantom{ }\\
                \begingroup

                \hline
                    Population mean & 2800\\
                    \hline
                    Standard deviation & 60\\
                    \hline

\phantomsection\label{ex133a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex133q]{Exercise 13.3}}{Exercise 13.3}}\label{exercise-13.3}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex133q]{Exercise 13.3}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{multicols}{2}
        \begin{enumerate}
            \item Population proportion $=0.6$\\
            Sample size $=24$\\[0.2em]
                \begingroup
                \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}
                \begin{tabular}{|r|l|}
                \hline
                    Upper Control Limit & 0.9\\
                    \hline
                    $+2\sigma$ Limit & 0.8\\
                    \hline
                    $+1\sigma$ Limit & 0.7\\
                    \hline
                    Target Value & 0.6\\
                    \hline
                    $-1\sigma$ Limit & 0.5\\
                    \hline
                    $-2\sigma$ Limit & 0.4\\
                    \hline
                    Lower Control Limit & 0.3\\
                    \hline
                \end{tabular}
                \endgroup
            \item Population proportion $=0.16$\\
            Sample size $=50$\\[0.2em]
                \begingroup
                \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}
                \begin{tabular}{|r|l|}
                \hline
                    Upper Control Limit & 0.316\\
                    \hline
                    $+2\sigma$ Limit & 0.264\\
                    \hline
                    $+1\sigma$ Limit & 0.212\\
                    \hline
                    Target Value & 0.16\\
                    \hline
                    $-1\sigma$ Limit & 0.108\\
                    \hline
                    $-2\sigma$ Limit & 0.056\\
                    \hline
                    Lower Control Limit & 0.004\\
                    \hline
                \end{tabular}
                \endgroup
        \end{enumerate}
    \end{multicols}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The samples of mugs would not be random. Also, for appropriate use of a $p$ chart it is required that $np>5$ and $nq>5$. Here, $0.08\times 30=2.4<5$. 
        \item Upper 2-sigma limit = 0.14265 and lower 2-sigma limit = 0.01735.
        \item The process is under statistical control for the samples shown, as none of the WECO rules have been violated.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $0.11+3\times\sqrt{\dfrac{0.11\times 0.89}{50}}=0.2427$
        \item The calculated lower limit would be -0.0227. However, a negative sample proportion is not possible, so the lower control limit is set as 0.
        \item The process is out of statistical control since, from samples 3 to 7, four out of five samples lie below the lower 2-sigma limit.
        \item Since these sample proportions suggest less seeds aere failing to germinate than expected, which is a good thing, the company many not be concerned.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item UCL = 0.9549, LCL = 0.6451.
        \item The process is out of statistical control since, from February to September, eight consecutive sample proportions fall below the target value.
        \item The sample size needs to be at least 30 to ensure both $np>5$ and $nq>5$.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\hat{p}=0.12$
        \item Upper 2-sigma limit $=0.12+2\times\sqrt{\dfrac{0.12\times0.88}{50}}=0.212$ and lower 2-sigma limit $=0.12-2\times\sqrt{\dfrac{0.12\times0.88}{50}}=0.028$
        \item The process is under statistical control for the samples shown, as none of the WECO rules have been violated.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The population proportion is $p=0.6$.
        \item UCL = 0.9 and LCL = 0.3.
        \item The sample size is $n=24$.
        \item The sample size and population proportion are appropriate since both of  $24\times 0.6=14.4$ and $24\times 0.4 = 9.6$ are greater than $5$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{re13a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[re13q]{Chapter 13 Review Exercise}}{Chapter 13 Review Exercise}}\label{chapter-13-review-exercise}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[re13q]{Chapter 13 Review Exercise}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Upper 2-sigma limit $=60.12$ and lower 2-sigma limit $=55.88$.
        \item The process is out of statistical control since the sample mean is below the lower control limit at sample 14.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Upper 1-sigma limit $=0.926$ and lower 1-sigma limit $=0.834$.
        \item $0.88\leqslant \hat{p} \leqslant 1$
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\subsection*{Chapter 16 - Wilcoxon Signed Rank Test}\label{chapter-16---wilcoxon-signed-rank-test}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Chapter 16 - Wilcoxon Signed Rank Test}

\begingroup
\footnotesize

\phantomsection\label{ex161a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex161q]{Exercise 16.1}}{Exercise 16.1}}\label{exercise-16.1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex161q]{Exercise 16.1}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $9>3\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $5.5<3\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $8>5\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $7.5>5\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $10\leqslant 10\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $9<10\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $6.5>5\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
\end{enumerate}

    \item $11>5\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $46>21\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $1.5<2\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $4<8\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
\end{enumerate}

    \item $-0.34>-1.96\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $-1.67<-1.64\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $-0.27>-1.64\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $H_0: \eta =110, \quad H_1: \eta<110$
        \item $-2.16>-1.64\implies\text{reject }H_0$
        \item Assume that time taken to complete the hike is symmetrically distributed.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\subsection*{Chapter 17 - Mann Whitney Rank Sum Test}\label{chapter-17---mann-whitney-rank-sum-test}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{Chapter 17 - Mann Whitney Rank Sum Test}

\begingroup
\footnotesize

\phantomsection\label{ex171a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex171q]{Exercise 17.1}}{Exercise 17.1}}\label{exercise-17.1-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex171q]{Exercise 17.1}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Rank sum of 20.5, or rank sum of 44.5 leading to smaller value of 20.5.
        \item $20.5<21\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item $65.5<78\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $28\leqslant28\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $27.5<31\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \item $29>28\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item Assume distributions of learning times for each type of mouse have the same shape and variance.\\
    $38.5>26\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\text{H}_0:\eta_A=\eta_B$ and $\text{H}_1:\eta_A\ne\eta_B$
        \item $72.5>73\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
        \item Assume the distributions of mass of dust deposited for each boiler type have the same shape and variance. Assume the samples of boilers are random. Assume the samples are independent.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex172a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex172q]{Exercise 17.2}}{Exercise 17.2}}\label{exercise-17.2-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex172q]{Exercise 17.2}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $-2.39<-2.33\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
        \item Assume that the distributions of driving times for each weather condition have the same shape and variance.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item $-1.47>-1.96\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item $-1.96>-1.64\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The distributions of gradings for each line appear to have the same shape and variance, to a Mann-Whitney test is appropriate. The distributions do not appear to be normal, so a $t$-test would not be appropriate.
        \item $-1.34<-1.28\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex173a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex173q]{Exercise 17.3}}{Exercise 17.3}}\label{exercise-17.3-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex173q]{Exercise 17.3}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item 56
        \item $\{1,2,3\},\{1,2,4\},\{1,2,5\},\{1,3,4\}$
        \item $\frac{4}{56}=\frac{1}{14}\text{ or }0.0714$
    \end{enumerate}
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item 126
        \item $\{1,2,3,4\},\{1,2,3,5\},\{1,2,3,6\},\{1,2,4,5\}$
        \item $\frac{4}{126}=\frac{2}{63}\text{ or }0.0317$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $1+2+4+8=15$
        \item $\{1,2,3,4\},\{1,2,3,5\},\{1,2,3,6\},\{1,2,3,7\},\{1,2,3,8\}$\\
        $\{1,2,3,9\},\{1,2,4,5\},\{1,2,4,6\},\{1,2,4,7\},\{1,2,4,8\}$\\
        $\{1,2,5,6\},\{1,2,5,7\},\{1,3,4,5\},\{1,3,4,6\},\{1,3,4,7\}$\\
        $\{1,3,5,6\},\{2,3,4,5\},\{2,3,4,6\}$
        \item Assume that the population distributions of reactions times for each group have the same shape and variance.
        \item $\frac{18}{330}=\frac{3}{55}\text{ or }0.0545$
        \item $0.0545>0.05\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\frac{7}{495}=0.0141$
        \item $0.0141<0.1\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
        \item Assume that the samples of hockey and rugby players are random.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{re17a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[re17q]{Chapter 17 Review Exercise}}{Chapter 17 Review Exercise}}\label{chapter-17-review-exercise}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[re17q]{Chapter 17 Review Exercise}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $34>29\implies\text{ do not reject }H_0$
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The stem-and-leaf diagram shows that red wines seem to contain more alcohol on average than white wines.
        \item $-3.04<-1.64\implies\text{ reject }H_0$
        \item Since the stem-and-leaf diagram seems to suggest that the distributions of alcohol content for the two groups have the same shape and variance, a Mann-Whitney test would be appropriate. Since the distributions both appear to be normal-like, a $t$-test would also be appropriate.
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $H_0:\;\eta_A = \eta_B$ and $H_0:\;\eta_A \ne \eta_B$
        \item CV$=13$. Since $12<13$ reject $H_0$ at the 2\% significance level, so there is evidence to suggest that the two populations do not have the same median.
        \item Assuming that the samples are random, and assume that the populations have the same shape and variance.
        \item The data for samples A and B will have been combined and ordered, with ranks assigned to each value. The rank sum for sample A will have been found, $W_A$, and then the value $4\times (4+9+1)-W_A$ will have also been calculated. \texttt{W=12} will have been the smaller of those two values.
    \end{enumerate}
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex182a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex182q]{Exercise 18.2}}{Exercise 18.2}}\label{exercise-18.2-1}

            \item $s=3.05$

    \end{multicols}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The scatterplot suggests there is a strong negative linear association between the distance a runner covers in training in the four weeks before a marathon and their finishing time.
        \item $s^2=10.97$
        \item $\hat{Y}=194.8-23.48x$
        \item $r=-0.928$\newline The correlation coefficient also suggests there is a strong negative linear association between the distance a runner covers in training in the four weeks before a marathon and their finishing time.
        \item Fitted $=112.6$, residual $=-2.6$
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex183a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex183q]{Exercise 18.3}}{Exercise 18.3}}\label{exercise-18.3-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex183q]{Exercise 18.3}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $R^2=0.775$. This suggests that 77.5\% of the variation in $Y$ is explained by the linear model.
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $R^2=0.734$. This suggests that 73.4\% of the variation in the exmployees' salaries is explained by the linear model.\newline (Or: \textit{...by the number of years they have been employed for}).
        \item $R^2=0.573$. This suggests that 57.3\% of the variation in red squirrel mass is explained by the linear model.\newline (Or: \textit{...by the height of the red squirrels}).
        \item $R^2=0.449$. This suggests that 44.9\% of the variation in the top speed reached is explained by the linear model.\newline (Or: \textit{...by their lap time}).
    \end{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The scatterplot appears to show a moderate/strong, positive linear association between a camera's shutter count and age.
        \item The correlation coefficient also suggests there is a moderate/strong, positive linear association between a camera's shutter count and its age.
        \item $R^2=0.516$. This suggests that 51,6\% of the variation in a camera's shutter count is explained by the linear model.\newline (Or: \textit{...by its age}).
        \item Since the data collected only includes digital cameras from within the last 12 years, any results from it may not apply to much older film cameras.\newline Also, the regression line is $y$ on $x$, suitable only for predicting shutter count based on a camera's age.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\phantomsection\label{ex184a}{}

\subsubsection*{\texorpdfstring{\hyperref[ex184q]{Exercise 18.4}}{Exercise 18.4}}\label{exercise-18.4-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsubsection}{\hyperref[ex184q]{Exercise 18.4}}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\text{H}_0$: $\beta=0$ \newline $\text{H}_1$: $\beta\ne 0$
        \item $s^2 = \dfrac{224.2}{27-2}=8.968,\:s = \sqrt{8.987}=2.995,\:b = \dfrac{-129.6}{87.8}=-1.476,\:t = \dfrac{-1.476\times \sqrt{87.8}}{2.995}=-4.618$
        \item $\nu=27-2=25$
        \item Critical value for $\nu=25$ and a 5\% two tailed test (and a negative test statistic) is $t_{25,0.975}=-2.060$.\newline Since $-4.618<-2.060$ reject $\text{H}_0$ at the 5\% level of significance.\newline There is evidence to suggest that the slope parameter $\beta\ne 0$, and so the linear model is useful for prediction.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item Test statistic $t=-5.120$, critical value $t_{32,0.95}=-1.694$, reject $H_0$ since $-5.120<-1.694$.
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\text{H}_0$: $\beta=0$ \newline $\text{H}_1$: $\beta\ne 0$
        \item $n=40$
        \item Critical value $t_{0.975,38}=-2.024$. Since $-0.42295>-2.024$, do not rejecrt $H_0$ at the 5\% significance level. There is insufficient evidence to suggest that the slope parameter $\beta$ is not equal to zero, and so no evidence that the linear model is useful for prediction.
        \item Assume that the population of errors are independent and identically distributed as $\text{N}(0,\sigma^2)$.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item Test statistic $t=-13.458$, critical value $t_{68,0.995}=-2.576$, reject $H_0$ since $-13.458<-2.576$.
    \item Test statistic $t=0.013$, critical value $t_{6,0.975}=2.447$, do not reject $H_0$ at the 5\% level since $0.013<2.447$.
\end{enumerate}

        \item 
        \begin{flalign*}
            t &= \dfrac{-0.678\sqrt{27-2}}{\sqrt{1-(-0.678)^2}}=-4.612&&
        \end{flalign*}
        \item $\nu=27-2=25$
        \item Critical value for $\nu=25$ and a 5\% two tailed test (and a negative test statistic) is $t_{25,0.975}=-2.060$.\newline Since $-4.612<-2.060$ reject $\text{H}_0$ at the 5\% level of significance.\newline There is evidence to suggest that there is a linear association between the variables.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item Test statistic $t=6.418$, critical value $t_{12,0.95}=1.782$, reject $H_0$ at the 10\% level since $6.418>1.782$.

\end{enumerate}

        \item There appears to be a weak positive linear association between `rating' and `$\log_{10}\text{energy}$'.\newline The coefficient of determination suggests 38.2\% of the variation in `$\log_2{\text{energy}}$' is explained by the model.
        \item There appears to be a very strong positive linear association between `$\log_2\text{time}$' and `$\log_2\text{population}$'.\newline The coefficient of determination suggests 97.4\% of the variation in `$\log_2{\text{population}}$' is explained by the model.
        \item There appears to be no linear association between `quality' and `$\dfrac{1}{\text{cost}}$'.\newline The coefficient of determination suggests 0.1\% of the variation in `$\dfrac{1}{\text{cost}}$' is explained by the model.
    \end{enumerate}

        \item $\widehat{\dfrac{1}{Y}}=2.936+2.898x$
        \item 0.0574
    \end{enumerate}
    \item $r=-0.984$. This suggests that there is a very strong negative linear correlation between $\log x$ and $\log y$.
    \item 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item They would have observed the points not showing an equal variance as fitted values increased, such as a funnel shape.
        \item They should use model C, as it is the only one whose residual plot appears to show a random scatter of points centred on zero, with a constant variance as fitted values increase. This suggests it is the only model of the three which is appropriate.
    \end{enumerate}
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The residual plot appears to show a curved pattern, suggesting a linear model between $p$ and $x$ is not appropriate.
        \item The residual plot for this new model appears to show a random scatter of points centred around zero, with a constant variance, so this model appears to be appropriate.
        \item $\hat{Y}=0.0598+8.85x$
        \item 10.3
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}
